Optimization of Damping in Flexible
Rotor-Bearing Systems via
Controllable Fluid Film Bearings
Considering Thermal Eﬀects
Master’s thesis
Christian Svensson, s134159
1st of February, 2019
Technical University of Denmark
Department of Mechanical Engineering
Supervisor:
Professor, Dr.-Ing. dr.techn. livre-docente Ilmar Santos, DTU
==PAGE:3==
Preface
This thesis was written at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU), in the Department
of Mechanical Engineering, as a product of a 30 ECTS point project in fulﬁllment with the
requirements for obtaining an MSc.Eng. in Mechanical Engineering. The thesis was written
in the time between August 1st of 2018 and February 1st of 2019, under the supervision of
Professor, Dr.-Ing. dr.techn. livre-docente Ilmar Ferreira Santos.
The thesis deals with the ﬂuid mechanics, thermal dynamics and machinery dynamics of
tilted pad journal bearings. As well as the numerical implementation and solution of such
subjects.
i
==PAGE:4==
Abstract
This master’s thesis is a continuation, in the process of developing a state of the art Mat-
lab programme, for determining the dynamic aspects of an actively lubricated tilted pad
journal bearing. The programme has been continuously expanded upon by previous mas-
ters students, since its initial development by Ilmar Ferreira Santos in 1998. The goal of
this thesis were to modify the latest iteration of the programme, to take the dissipation of
energy into heat in the lubricant, into consideration. Essentially changing the programme
from calculating on a simpliﬁed case of isothermal bearings, to more physically accurate
thermohydrodynamic bearings.
With the adjustments for the thermohydrodynamics, Allaire’s perturbation method which
had previously been used to make dynamic analyses on the isothermal system was revealed
to no longer yield trust-worthy results. As a results an alternative was implemented, utilising
perturbation theory to yield the necessary dynamics for carrying on.
Finally the same analyses that had been made for the isothermal system, could be repeated
for the thermohydrodynamic system. For conventional lubrication, the bearing which had
previously been deemed the most optimal under isothermal assumptions, was again chosen
as the optimal choice out of the four tested bearings. With hybrid lubrication the method
used for determining the temperature distribution, based on the energy equation, revealed
its limitations. This meant the quantity of results here were sparse, not at all enough to
make a reasonable proposal for optimisation via active lubrication. The analyses ultimately
resulted in the conclusion: that when running at high rotational speeds the impact of ther-
mohydrodynamics were too signiﬁcant to ignore, but that more work was necessary to model
it accurately with hybrid lubrication.
ii

entiﬁc background, proved an exceptional listener, when I needed to express my frustrations

==PAGE:12==
1
Introduction
This master’s thesis is a continuation in developing and improving a state of the art pro-
gramme, for evaluating the dynamics of tilted pad journal bearings (TPJB). The TPJB
being a very characteristic sort of hydrodynamic journal bearing, with the load carrying oil
ﬁlm ﬂowing between the journal and a set number of curved pads in the bearing. As the
name suggests, those pads are capable of tilting on a pivot, giving the TPJB a higher number
of degrees of freedom (DOF) than other journal bearings. The programme which has been
evolving continually, since its initial development at the hands of professor Ilmar Ferreira
Santos in 1998, was most recently updated by Suzan Kurt in relation to her master’s thesis
[1]. Kurt implemented the ﬂow of oil in and out of the oil ﬁlm at each pad, as well as the loss of
power due to viscous friction in the oil. And analysed the Campbell diagrams, stability maps
and unbalance responses in an attempt to optimize the controllable injection of oil in hybrid
lubrication of the TPJBs. All of the work conducted by Kurt with the TPJB programme,
as well as that of Mik Mønsted in relation to his master’s thesis [2], and others prior to him,
was done under the assumption of isothermal conditions for the ﬂuid mechanics of the TPJB.
The goal of this master’s thesis is to change that very fact, by reformulating the Reynold’s
equation, such that the viscosity in it is temperature dependant. And determine the temper-
ature distribution by means of applying conservation of energy and formulating the energy
equation. Which can then be implemented into the programme and solved numerically. Hav-
ing modiﬁed the programme to consider thermal eﬀects, all of the same analyses conducted
by Kurt is to be repeated, to identify whether the assumption of isothermal conditions is
viable to some extent, or not at all. The analyses conducted here are on the lateral dynamics
of the ﬂexible rotor-bearing system provided by General Electric, representing that of their
radial compressor . And ultimately those analyses should enable optimization of the control-
lable injection pressure, aiming at increasing the damping ratio of the industrial compressor.
To conduct the same analyses as in [1], a lot of the same theory needs to be described
for those analyses to make sense. As such, some sections merely summarizes theory that
couldn’t be described better than in the literature, which will be cited appropriately.
2
Tilting Pad Journal Bearing (TPJB)
The bearings considered in this project are all of the type ”Tilting Pad Journal Bearing” or
TPJB for short. A kind of hydrodynamic bearing wherein the load carrying lubricant ﬂows
as a thin oil ﬁlm, between the rotating journal and the bearing pads. While the bearing
pads to some extend can be considered stationary, the pads of a TPJB diﬀers from those
of a conventional journal bearing in that they have a rotational degree of freedom around a
pivot, enabling them to tilt as the bearing name suggests.
1
==PAGE:13==
Figure 1: Kinematics of the jth pad, from [3].
In Figure 1 above, from Santos and Russo’s ’98 article, a journal with a single pad is depicted,
illustrating the ﬁrst three reference frames used when considering the system.
1. The inertial frame I(X, Y, Z), with the origin O at the center of the bearing.
2. The auxiliary frame Bj(x, y, z), with the origin in the contact point between the jth
pad and its pivot, rotated relative to the inertial frame with the position angle of the
jth pad φ.
3. The moving frame B′
j(x′, y′, z′), which is rotated relative to the auxiliary frame with
the pivot angle α.
A fourth frame is also considered, where the tangential axis follows the curvature of the pad.
This is necessary when working with the ﬂuid mechanics of the oil ﬁlm and is illustrated in
Figure 2.
4. The curvelinear frame ¯Bj(¯x, ¯y, ¯z), with its origin translated along x′, relative to the
moving frame origin, by an amount equal to minus the pad thickness, −∆.
Working in the curvelinear reference frame, the oil ﬁlm thickness can be determined as
formulated by Springer in [4]:
h(¯y, t) = C0 + C1(t) cos

Rs


Where h is the ﬁlm thickness, t is the time and Rs is the radius of curvature of the pad
surface. And the constant C0 and time dependant coeﬃcients C1(t) and S1(t) are deﬁned
as:
C0 = Rs −R
(2)
C1(t) = [α(t)(Rs + ∆) + yJ(t) −η(t)] sin(φs) −[C0 −h0 + xJ(t) −ξ(t)] cos(φs)
(3)
S1(t) = −[α(t)(Rs + ∆) + yJ(t) −η(t)] cos(φs) −[C0 −h0 + xJ(t) −ξ(t)] sin(φs)
(4)
2
==PAGE:14==
Wherein R is the radius of the journal, ∆is the pad thickness and h0 is the initial ﬁlm
thickness.
The ﬁlm thickness function as it is expressed in (1) to (4) above, diﬀers from that presented
by Springer in [4] by including the constant φs, which is the angular distance from the contact
point between pad and pivot, to the middle of the pad. If said contact point is at the middle
of the pad φs = 0, the ﬁlm thickness function simpliﬁes to that presented by Springer. This
ﬂuid ﬁlm function was determined suﬃciently precise compared to the ”exact” ﬁlm thickness,
by Mønsted in [2].
3
Fluid mechanics
In this section the ﬂuid mechanics of the programme is presented. This is where the theory
diﬀers, from the corresponding isothermal (ISO) theory used in the programme up until now.
In the following subsections, a modiﬁed thermohydrodynamic (THD) Reynold’s equation, as
well as a simpliﬁed energy equation will be formulated. And how each of those are discretized,
implemented and solved will be described in detail.
3.1
Reynold’s equation
Figure 2: Section of journal and pad surfaces with kinematic properties of the ﬂuid ﬁlm in
between, from [3].
The Reynold’s equation used to determine the pressure in the ﬂuid ﬁlm is a modiﬁcation of
the reduced Navier-Stokes equation, deﬁned as:

ﬁeld in the x, y and z directions respectively. kc is the oil thermal conductivity and Cv is the
speciﬁc heat of the oil, deﬁned as the change in thermal energy with respect to the change
in temperature.
Considering the change of temperature in time is nonsensical, as the time domain when
considering stability is in the range of microseconds, and in such a domain any change in
temperature of the oil is negligible. So ∂T
∂t = 0.
Instead, due to the moving ﬂuid inducing forced convection, another term for the convection
is necessary. Following the example of [6] that can be expressed as:
ρCp

diﬀusion from (25).

Where L¯z is the length of the pad in the ¯z-direction. The resulting temperature at the inlet

And Tout(j−1) is the temperature at the outlet edge of pad (j −1) determined by solving the
energy equation for that pad.
3.3
Discretization and Implementation
Both the modiﬁed Reynold’s equation and energy equation are non-linear, and both have
to be solved numerically. To do so they ﬁrst need to be discretized. While the procedure
for discretizing each problem are similar, they are not the same, as the Reynold’s equation
is solved by approximating the ﬁrst derivatives of p as a central diﬀerence between known
values, while the energy equation is solved by approximating the ﬁrst derivatives of T as a
forward diﬀerence, a so called ”upwind scheme”. The diﬀerence of which should be apparent
following this section.
When discretizing the pad, the assumptions that the ﬂow is uniform, and that the gra-
dient of the ﬁlm thickness with respect to ¯z is negligible, means the behavior of the ﬂuid ﬁlm
is symmetric about the ¯y axis. So instead of discretizing the entire pad just half the pad is
discretized. Mønsted concluded in [2] that a mesh consisting of 60 nodes in each direction
of the entire pad, was suﬃcient for the results to converge. In that case, half the pad is
discretized by dividing it into pl = 60 nodes in the range [−Ly/2, Ly/2] and into pc = 30
nodes in the range [0, Lz/2] in the ¯y and ¯z-directions respectively. With Ly and Lz being the
lengths of the whole pad in the two directions respectively. The pressure and temperature
distribution across the pad is then determined by calculating a value for each at every node,
through discretizing and solving the Reynold’s and energy equations respectively.
3.3.1
Solving Reynold’s equation
Using a central diﬀerence scheme, the ﬁrst derivatives of the pressure p can be approximated
by:
∂p
∂¯y = pm+1,n −pm−1,n
2∆¯y
(34)
∂p
∂¯z = pm,n+1 −pm,n−1
2∆¯z
(35)
Where the subscripts m and n indicate at which node the approximation is being evalu-
ated, with m changing with movement in ¯y and n with movement in ¯z. ∆¯y and ∆¯z express
8
==PAGE:20==
the distance between two nodes in the ¯y- and ¯z-direction respectively. The expressions ar-
gue that the derivative of p with respect to ¯y, at a node [m, n] can be approximated by
the diﬀerence in pressure between the two adjacent nodes in the ¯y direction ([m + 1, n] and
[m−1, n]), divided by the distance between those two nodes. And likewise for the ¯z-direction.
The second derivatives of p can be approximated in a similar manner, by:
∂2p
∂¯y2 = pm+1,n + pm−1,n −2pm,n
∆¯y2
(36)
∂2p
∂¯z2 = pm,n+1 + pm,n−1 −2pm,n
∆¯z2
(37)
By inserting the approximations for the ﬁrst and second derivatives of p from (34) to (37) into
the reduced Reynold’s equation as expressed in (23), it can be formulated as the following
expression:

(38)
While the Reynold’s equation looks signiﬁcantly more complex as it’s presented in (38) than
it was previously, this form is considerably easier to solve as it’s now linear. With it, the
pressure at each node in the mesh can be evaluated, by making a system of linear equations.
For the example of a half pad mesh of pl × pc = 60 × 30, the system will consist of a matrix
A of size 1800 × 1800, and two vectors of size 1800 × 1: p wherein the elements are the
solution to the system and c wherein the elements are the right-hand-side of the Reynold’s
equation (38).
A · p = c
(39)
The matrix A will consist of all the terms on the left-hand-side of equation (38), build in a
manner such that each cell will either hold a zero, or one of the parentheses from (38). Which
parentheses a cell holds, if it holds any, depends on the cell. As it has to be organized such
that when solving the system of equations, the resulting elements in the vector p corresponds
to the pressure at each node in the mesh. By considering the simple case of a plate discretized
with a total of just 15 nodes, as depicted in Figure 3 below, matrix A is of the size 15 × 15
and can be written out.

Similarly to how Russo formulated it in [7]. It’s worth mentioning that in every column of

in the ¯y-direction, there are 5 −2 = 3 zeros between ♦and ♠in every column, and ♥and
♣in every row.
The TPJB programme is constructed such that a whole plate needs to be discretized with
the same amount of nodes in ¯z and ¯y to work - meaning the half plate, which is considered
during numerical calculations, need exactly half as many nodes in ¯z as in ¯y. So the 5 × 3
nodes plate, used in the example above is for visualisation purposes only and wouldn’t be a
viable discretization in the programme. Also, in the TPJB programme, the mesh is extended
with two extra nodes in the ¯y direction, and one in the ¯z direction, where the pressures are
predeﬁned as 0 (atmospheric).

the previous section, for each node at the edge of the inlet of pad j.
3.4
Programme
Santos and Nicoletti describes the numerical procedure in [8] with an illustration similar to
Figure 4. This almost perfectly describes the method with which the new programme will
determine position, temperature and viscosity distributions. Instead of using the Newton-
Raphson method however, the ”trust region dogleg” method is used, as it was implemented
and used by Mønsted in [2], and subsequently by Kurt in [1].
12
==PAGE:24==
Begin
Initial values X0, T0, µ0
Reynold’s equation
solution to the j’th pad
Pressure and
hydrodynamic forces
Forces and moments balance
X
||X0 −X|| < tol
Pad inlet temperature
Energy equation
solution to the j’th pad
T
||T0 −T|| < tol
µ = µ(T)
||µ0 −µ|| < tol
End
T0 = T
µ0 = µ
X0 = X
Trust region dogleg method
j = [1..ns]
j = [1..ns]
Figure 4: Flowchart of the numerical procedure
As is illustrated in Figure 4, initial values for the temperature and viscosity of the lubricant
is given by the programme user. An initial value for the position is likewise given, if not by
the user then automatically (one of the beneﬁts with the Trust Region method, as opposed
13
==PAGE:25==
to Newton-Raphson). The programme starts two loops, the ﬁrst of which won’t end until
the viscosity is converged, and the second one, nested inside the ﬁrst, is to determine the
pressure distribution, forces and equilibrium position based on Reynold’s equation. When
the equilibrium position is determined for the given viscosity a third loop is initiated, still
nested within the ﬁrst, with the goal of determining the temperature distribution based on
the Energy equation. With the third loop converged, and the temperature determined, the
viscosity is redetermined based on this temperature distribution. The ﬁrst loop then con-
tinues iterating, with the two nested loops inside, until the viscosity of the lubricant has
converged.
By discretizing and solving the energy equation as described previously, and looping over
not only the equilibrium position and temperature but the viscosity as well, the new model
should be closer to reality than previous iterations. Whether or not this is true however, is
yet to be tested. Before testing if it’s better than before, the new model has to be validated to
see whether it’s any good at all. Ideally this would be done by running the programme with
a set of bearing data, at diﬀerent velocities with diﬀerent levels of injection pressure, which
could all be compared to experimental results. As experimental results are of course the
closest we get to reality, despite the risk of experimental uncertainties. The new programme
however shows limitations where there previously were none, which resulted in diﬃculties
with ﬁnding any experimental results to compare with.
3.4.1
Validation
As it wasn’t possible to test against experimental results, as no comparable results could
be found, the next best thing was used. The programme written by Santos and used in [9]
was itself validated against experimental results, and used as reference in numerous other
scientiﬁc papers. Minor corrections were made to this old programme, as well as to the
new one, to enable comparative results. E.g. how the viscosity was determined from the
temperature, or how the height of the oil ﬁlm was calculated. With such small corrections to
each programme, all that was needed was for the bearing data to be the same. By making
a bearing data ﬁle for the new programme, with the same parameters as those used by the
old, the results when simulating were comparative and the new model could be validated.
Parameters that were made identical to assure as similar running conditions as possible, were
such as mesh, rotor load, geometry (e.g. radii of rotor and pads, relative positions, preload
factor) as well as lubricant characteristics (e.g. viscosity and density).
In Table 2 below, results are presented for a run with no injection pressure at six diﬀer-
ent values for the angular velocity, ranging from 3000 to 15000 RPM. The results consist of
the equilibrium position YJ, as well as maximum pressure and temperature for each pad (of
which there are four), presented for both the new programme and the old, along with the
deviation of the new from the old, in percentages.

The results as they are presented in Table 2, are considered suﬃcient to deem the new pro-

at 7800 RPM. As mentioned previously only minor changes were made to the old programme,
none of which changed the workings of it but merely made the results from it and the new
programme comparative. With the results from the new programme being so close to those
of a programme which has been validated, the new one can be considered validated as well.
Furthermore the results presented in Table 2 is only one set out of multiple. The rest of
the results for the comparison are presented in Appendix A. The ﬁrst of which are results
15
==PAGE:27==
for isotherm conditions, where the maximum deviation reaches just 0.01 %, the second are
the same as in Table 2 for non-isotherm but without injection, the third and fourth sets
are also for non-isotherm conditions, but with 5 bar injection through one and ﬁve oriﬁces
respectively, non of which contains a deviation value higher than the 0.67 % in Table 2.
The changes that were made to the new programme, solely for the sake of this compari-
son, will be rolled back to their previous states. The reason for this being that the previous
state has already been validated by Kurt in [1] against Someya, for isotherm conditions.
Keeping the new programme as it was when comparing to the old programme from Santos
[9], wouldn’t ruin that validation but merely increase the deviation from Someya slightly.
Just like the deviations from Santos would increase slightly if the changes hadn’t been im-
plemented.
Rolling back some of the changes that were made for the validation of the
programme, is done so to ensure that it’s in the best possible state moving forward.
4
Rotor Dynamics
The theory presented in this chapter is that behind the dynamics of the rotor. Nothing
in the modeling of the rotor has changed since the previous iterations of the programme,
developed by Kurt for, and described in, [1]. As no changes were made by the author of this
report, this chapter serves as a mere recap of the theory presented in [10].
The shaft is modeled in three dimensions as consisting of numerous beam-elements, where
every element has a node at each end. The nodes are then capable of translation along the
X and Y axes (XJ and YJ respectively), as well as rotation around the X and Y axes (βJ
and ΓJ respectively). With two nodes for each beam element, every element has a total of
8 DOFs denoted as qi where i ∈[1, 2, ..., 8].
16
==PAGE:28==
Figure 5: Beam element with 2 nodes, each with 4 DOFs (XJ, YJ, βJ and ΓJ
), giving a total of 8 DOFs (qi where i ∈[1, 2, ..., 8]). Figure from Kurt [1].
To describe the movement of any point in a beam-element, shape functions are formulated.
These describe the translational and rotational movement, of a point in the beam-element, at
a distance s from the end of the element. The translational and rotational shape functions,
where the latter is the ﬁrst derivative of the former with respect to s, are formulated as:
ψ(s) = A + Bs + Cs2 + Ds3 = qi
where
i = 1, 2, 5, 6
(45)
θ(s) = ∂φ(s)
∂s
= B + 2Cs + 3Ds2 = qi
where
i = 3, 4, 7, 8
(46)

tions. Resulting in the following translational and rotational shape functions:

The translational deformation inside the beam-element at s, in the XJ direction can then
be described as a linear combination between the four shape functions of translation in the
XZ-plane, and the movement of the nodes at the ends of the element.
XJ(s, t) = ψ1(s)q1(t) + ψ2(s)q4(t) + ψ3(s)q5(t) + ψ4(s)q8(t)
(51)

YJ(s, t) = ψ1(s)q2(t) −ψ2(s)q3(t) + ψ3(s)q6(t) −ψ4(s)q7(t)

4.1
Discs
The rotor will have impellers attached at various placements along its length, these impellers
are modelled as rigid discs attached to particular rotor nodes. Taking the same approach as
previously done with the rotor dynamics: Solving Lagrange’s equation for the four degrees
of freedom of a disc (XJ, YJ, βJ and ΓJ), four equations of motion can be formulated which
rewritten in matrix form has just two matrices of dynamic coeﬃcient, as the assumption
of rigidity means the disc can’t store potential energy - meaning it has no stiﬀness. The
remaining dynamic matrices from the equations of motion of the disc are its mass and
20
==PAGE:32==
gyroscopic matrices, Md and Gd respectively:
Md =

Where md is the mass of the disc, Id is its transverse mass moment of inertia and Ip is its
polar mass moment of inertia.
When assembled with the rotor dynamics, these matrices are added to the mass and gy-
roscopic matrices of the node, at which the disc is then attached.
4.2
Bearings
The TPJB dynamics, when determined, will be added to the global system, by adding the
damping and stiﬀness coeﬃcients related to the DOFs of the journal, to the corresponding
matrix elements of the rotor nodes at which the bearings are to be attached. Determining
the dynamics of the TPJBs however proved more troublesome when considering THD than
initially expected. All of which is explained in detail in the next section.
4.3
Discretization and assembly
In [2] it’s explained how the rotor code used in the TPJB programme was supplied, compli-
ments to one Ph.D. student Jorge Andr´es Gonz´alez Salazar. Said code is a FEM model of
the 2050 mm long shaft, discretized as 18 elements, dimensionally mirrored around the shaft
center. As illustrated in Figure 7.

Figure 7: Rotor discretization with red dots illustrating node locations along the shaft.
Figure from Mønsted [2].

of all the shaft elements are as listed in Table 3.

As is depicted in Figure 7, there are six discs mounted onto the shaft on each side of its
center. With the disc on node 3 on the LHS being slightly wider than the others (mounted
on nodes 4, 5, 7, 8 and 9 on the LHS), with a width of 100 mm as opposed to their 55 mm.
All discs have the same outer radius of 221 mm, and assumed made of steel with a density
of ρ = 7800 kg/m3, Young’s modulus of E = 210 GPa and Poisson’s ratio of ν = 0.3.
5
TPJB Dynamics
An essential part of the stability analysis of the TPJB, is to determine the dynamic coeﬃ-
cients of the system. Namely the stiﬀness and damping coeﬃcients. This can be done by
22
==PAGE:34==
means of diﬀerent methods, one of which is the one presented by Allaire in [11], which not
only provides the dynamic coeﬃcients associated with the displacement and velocity of the
journal, but also those associated with the degrees of freedom of the pads. The high velocities
and forces in the TPJBs of this system necessitates the latter to be included in the analysis
as well. The TPJB programme is capable of providing the reduced dynamic coeﬃents, as
that has previously been a useful feature, as exempliﬁed by Kurt in the validation of the
previous version of the programme in [1]. The new TPJB programme however has already
been validated, and the reduced dynamic coeﬃcients are no longer of any interest.
5.1
Allaire’s perturbation method
At this point the equilibrium position of journal and bearings are determined at a given
angular velocity of the former, done iteratively as explained in the previous section and
illustrated in Figure 4. This equilibrium of journal and pads are upheld by the hydrodynamic
forces on them due to the pressure of the oil, forces that in the [x′, y′] referance frame will be
named Fx′
J, Fy′
J, Fξ′, Fη′ and Mα. The last of which is the moment on the pad in α, which
could just as easily be described as a linear force on the center of the pad arc in y′ times the
distance to the pivot, the arc radius Rs + ∆.
Figure 8: Perturbation of journal and pad in 5 degrees of freedom. Figure from Kurt [1]
.
The hydrodynamic forces on the journal and pads, can be modelled as linear springs and
dampers. Considering a system of a journal with a single pad, as depicted in Figure 8, spring
forces in x′ and y′ directions respectively, associated with the perturbation of the journal

5.2
Performance of Allaire’s method
Having assembled the full stiﬀness and damping matrices, it’s now possible to investigate the
eﬃciency of the perturbation method presented by Allaire. Allaire’s perturbation method
were initially chosen due to it’s consideration of the pad DOFs, and was proven very capable
of doing the job by Kurt in [1] where the resulting reduced dynamic coeﬃcients, calculated
on the basis of this method, were validated. The validation of the new programme however,
were carried out based on the resulting equilibrium position, pressure and temperature, and
therefore doesn’t prove whether Allaire’s perturbation method would still be viable.
Naturally; with the inclusion of the thermal eﬀect in the calculations, the perturbations
used for the Allaire method of determining dynamic coeﬃcients, has to be redetermined.
The procedure for this is by means of a convergence study. In Figure 9 below, such a study
is shown for ISO. With high values of perturbations in position (low values of x in δ = 10−x),
the stiﬀness coeﬃcients can’t be determined at all, and merely comes out as null. Decreasing
the size of the perturbation and an area of incoherent noise appears where the value of the
stiﬀness coeﬃcient clearly can’t be trusted. Then by decreasing the size of the perturbations
further, the stiﬀness coeﬃcient apparently converges to a stable reasonable value, within a
certain range of sizes of perturbations in position, before the size of the perturbations then
becomes too small and more noise appears. The damping coeﬃents converge at relatively
high values of the perturbation in velocity (low values of x in ˙δ = 10−x), and stays converged
in a large range of values of perturbation in velocity, before the perturbation becomes too
small and noise appear here as well.
5
10
15
0
5
10
107
0
5
10
15
1
1.5
2
2.5
105
Figure 9: Analysis of dynamic coeﬃcients for 4-pad LBP ISO, with change in perturbation
in position δ and velocity ˙δ respectively
27
==PAGE:39==
Considering the convergence analysis in Figure 9, the choices of δ = 10−11 and ˙δ = 10−7 seem
very reasonable. And a similar behavior is desired for the next analysis with THD settings:
a large range of sizes of perturbations in position as well as velocity, wherein any change in
dynamic coeﬃcients as a result of change in size of perturbation is negligible. Such that a
ﬁnite value for the size of the perturbations in position and velocity for THD calculations can
be determined with conﬁdence. In Figure 10 the same analysis is carried out as in Figure 9,
the only diﬀerence being that this is with thermal eﬀects taken into consideration. Clearly
there is no range of sizes of perturbations, wherein the dynamic coeﬃcients are converged.

Figure 10: Analysis of dynamic coeﬃcients for 4-pad LBP THD, with change in perturbation
in position δ and velocity ˙δ respectively
The method should be viable, but at this point it’s impossible to determine what the size
of the perturbations need to be, to get correct results.
So a way to carry on with the
convergence study, is to decrease the tolerance with wich the numeric solutions are accepted.
The programme works by using a set tolerance ”tol” to determine whether the nummerically
determined equilibrium position, temperature or viscosity from a given iteration is close
enough to that of the previous iteration to consider the result converged. In Figures 9 and
10 this tolerance were set to tol = 10−3 (carried over from the latest previous programme
version). Where the tolerance comes into play, can be seen in the ﬂow chart depicting the
numerical procedure, in Figure 4. While a tolerance of tol < 10−3 might have been overkill
for ISO, a decrease in tolerance is guarenteed to give more accurate results, and thereby a
clearer range of sizes of perturbations with which the dynamic coeﬃcients can be determined.
In Figure 11 below, the same analysis as in Figure 10 is carried out, but for a single velocity
(3000 RPM) with the tolerance varying from tol = 10−3 to tol = 10−10.
28
==PAGE:40==

Figure 11: Analysis of dynamic coeﬃcients for 4-pad LBP THD determined by Allaire’s
method, with change in perturbation in position δ and velocity ˙δ respectively, at 3000 RPM.
The convergence analyses depicted in Figure 11 above, was conducted at 3000 RPM as this
appeared to be the velocity from previous analyses at which the convergence in question was
most distinct. Considering the results, as presented in 11, there seems to be no improvement
when going from a tolerance of tol = 10−9 to tol = 10−10. This may be because the results
don’t get any better with a tolerance lower than tol = 10−9, or it may be an isolated case
like the one from tol = 10−5 to tol = 10−6, where there likewise doesn’t appear to be any
change.
Conducting similar analyses with higher rotational velocities revealed, that the dynamic co-
eﬃcients converge with the change in tolerance earlier for higher angular velocities than for
lower. To put it diﬀerently; for a high angular velocity, the point at which further dem-
inishing tol no longer aﬀects the range of values of perturbations, at which the dynamic
coeﬃcients are converged, occurs at a higher value of tol (a lower value of y in tol = 10−y)
than it does for lower angular velocities. At 15000 RPM for instance, a tolerance of less than
tol = 10−6 doesn’t improve the results at all.
But while the same point may occur at a lower value than tol = 10−10 for 3000 RPM, as
well as for even lower angular velocities, the programme is for some reason restricted to
a minimum tolerance of tol = 10−10. The cause for this restriction is unknown, and was
never investigated further for two reasons. 1. This is already a level of precision where one
can’t help but wonder; if this tolerance isn’t ﬁne enough, can it ever be? And 2. the main
interest of the project is to investigate the behavior at higher angular velocities, and in that
29
==PAGE:41==
endeavour a tolerance of tol = 10−10 was seen to be more than precise enough.
Going deeper into this analysis, it is revealed that while the dynamic coeﬃcients might
look somewhat converged for perturbations in the range of δ = [10−12..10−6] in position and
˙δ = [10−8..10−2] in velocity, the reality is entirely diﬀerent. In Figure 12a a zoom on the
converged area of the stiﬀness coeﬃcient from Figure 9 is depicted, here it is clear how a
perturbation in position of δ = 10−11 was a reasonable choice, as any minor change in size
of perturbation here has a negligible impact on the stiﬀness coeﬃcient, it can deﬁnitely be
considered converged. A similar zoom on the converged area of the stiﬀness coeﬃcient from
Figure 11, as presented in Figure 12b, reveals an optimal size of perturbation in position
of δ = 10−9 for THD, but a minor change in the perturbation size around this value has a
signiﬁcantly bigger impact on the stiﬀness coeﬃcient than in the case of ISO. As the eﬀect is
still fairly small, it may still be considered converged, but not to the same degree as it is for
ISO. Therefore a ﬁnal attempt was made to improve the model, namely by increasing the
discretization factors pl and pc, essentially making the mesh of the pad more dense. This of
course increases the amount of nodes at which the pressure, temperature and viscosity needs
to be evaluated, and thereby increases computational demand. The result of this attempt is
presented in Figure 12c below.

==PAGE:42==
While the increase in mesh density did seem to shift the curve downwards slightly, it had
no distinct impact on the ”ﬂatness” of the curve. Bearing in mind that these latest analyses
were conducted at an angular velocity of 3000 RPM, which was the ”ﬂattest” of the curves in
Figure 11, with higher angular velocities yielding more disheartening results, it is concluded
that Allaire’s perturbation method is not as precise for THD as it is for ISO, and to come
close: higher numerical precision is necessary, making it computationally demanding.
5.3
Perturbation theory
Another way of achieving the dynamic coeﬃcients of the TPJB, is by means of perturbation
theory. This is not to be confused with the theory presented in the previous section, based
on Allaire’s perturbation method. The diﬀerences between the two should become apparent
shortly.

The ﬁrst and second derivatives of the pressures with respect to ¯y or ¯z, due to some pertur-
bation, are determined using the same ﬁnite diﬀerence approximation described in Section
3.3.1, equations (34) to (37).
The expression in (94) is easily identiﬁed as being identical to the Reynold’s equation used
previously in determining the equilibrium position. To no surprise, this means the static
pressure at a pad, at the equilibrium position, is the same as the pressure previously deter-
mined at that pad to be necessary to uphold that very equilibrium position.
More interesting is it, that the left hand side of the following expressions in (95) to (99) are
likewise structured identically to the LHS in the reduced Reynold’s equation (88). Which
means: to solve those equations for the pressures induced by a perturbation in their respec-
tive directions (Pxj, Pyj, Pαj, Pξj and Pηj), the left-hand-sides are discretized in the same
manner as described in subsection 3.3.1. What is diﬀerent for the system of equations used
to solve for the dynamic pressures, compared to that used to solve for the static, is the
right-hand-side.

I(X, Y, Z). Just as one would do with coeﬃcients determined by means of Allaire’s pertur-
bation method. Having determined the dynamic coeﬃcients of the local rotor-pad system in
the initial referance frame, the procedure is repeated for each pad in the global system, and
the global stiﬀness and damping matrices are mapped out in the same way as one would do
with Allaire’s perturbation method.
Compared to Allaire’s perturbation method, this method of utilising the fundamentals of
perturbation theory may seem more convoluted. But while Allaire’s method is intuitive, it’s
a brute, and computationally more demanding, as will be evident in the following section.
5.4
Performance and Validation of Perturbation Theory
While the perturbation theory method, as described in the previous section, should be more
accurate and computationally less demanding than Allaire’s perturbation method, it ﬁrst
has to be validated. To that end, results obtained by means of perturbation theory needs
to be compared to trustworthy data. Kurt validated Allaire’s method against Someya in
[1], as such Allaire’s method should give correct results with isothermal conditions. And the
perturbation theory method can therefore be validated against such results.
It could be argued that the perturbation theory method should instead be validated against
Someya, like Allaire’s method was, thereby avoiding the risk of any chain of uncertainties
resulting in a type 2 error. While this was also the initial approach, it was dropped for two
reasons: to validate against Someya one needs to test with the same bearings as he has, and
at the same angular velocities. The ﬁrst is not a signiﬁcant issue, it just makes more sense
to validate with the bearings which one wishes to experiment with moving forward. The
latter is the main concern, as Someya’s results are given at relatively low velocities, which
for some reason is a problem when using perturbation theory.
So trusting in results for ISO conditions obtained using Allaire’s method, the perturbation
method is validated against such by running the programme for the four diﬀerent bearings
that will be tested further upon later: 4-pad LBP, 4-pad LOP, 5-pad LBP and 5-pad LOP.
The validation is carried out at three diﬀerent angular velocities for each bearing: a low,
a medium, and a high. It is noted in [1] that the programme has convergence issues, at
diﬀerent levels of low rotational speeds for the four diﬀerent bearings. As such the ”low”
velocities in the validation are the minimum velocities at which the programme doesn’t have
convergence issues for the diﬀerent bearings. The ”high” velocity is 15000 RPM, and the
”medium” velocity is the diﬀerence split in two.
The resulting tables of dynamic coeﬃcients for the four diﬀerent bearings, at three dif-
ferent velocities for each, are much too large to include in the report, but can be found in
Appendix C. Here it is evident that the results obtained by means of perturbation theory
matches those obtained using Allaire’s perturbation method. At least to some extent - the
only signiﬁcant deviations being in the 5-pad bearings at the low velocities, 800 and 500
RPM respectively. For both of those bearings, the high deviations at the low velocities are
notably only present for the dynamic coeﬃcients related to the DOFs of the lowest bearing
36
==PAGE:48==
pad(s), speciﬁcally pad 4 and 5 for the LBP bearing and pad 4 for the LOP bearing. These
speciﬁcs could indicate that the assumption made in the perturbation theory: that higher
order terms of perturbations are negligible, may not be entirely accurate. At low rotational
speeds, and at high loads, the oil ﬁlm thickness at the lowest bearing pads might be so thin,
that while the perturbations are small, they may not be small enough for all perturbation
terms of higher order than 1 to be negligible.
Whether this is indeed the cause for the higher deviations visible in the tables of Appendix
C can’t be said for sure. But though no other source for the deviations were found, the fact
remains that they only appear at very low rotational speeds, where the focus of this thesis
is at the high speeds. As such the method is considered validated, and will be used in all
further analyses.
The validation was carried out with a half-pad mesh of 60×30 nodes, a tolerance of tol = 10−3
for the numerics, and ofcourse ISO conditions. As Allaire’s method was previously proven
to work with such settings in [1]. With the perturbation theory method validated at such
settings, its beneﬁts with THD settings remains to be seen. In Figure 11 the dynamic coef-
ﬁcients from Allaire’s method, at a constant rotational velocity, did notably converge within
a range of perturbation values, with the decrease in tolerance (increase in y in tol = 10−y).
Assuming the value these converge towards is the correct result, a plot of the result from
perturbation theory under identical settings, should be placed at that value. In Figure 13
such a plot is presented.

The plots in Figure 13 above show that the dynamic coeﬃcients from Allaire’s perturbation
method do indeed converge against those from perturbation theory, within a range of per-
turbation values, with the decrease in tolerance tol. While there is but a single ”legend” for
the perturbation theory results (the grey line), there are in fact as many regressions for that
as there are for the Allaire method, they are simply so close to each other that the diﬀerence
37

is indistinguishable at this scale. And of course the size of perturbation doesn’t aﬀect the
results from perturbation theory, emphasizing a signiﬁcant advantage, in that an ideal value
for such a parameter doesn’t need to be identiﬁed to use this method.
While the diﬀerence in results from perturbation theory are indistinguishable in Figure 13,
that diﬀerence becomes more distinct at higher rotational speeds. In Figure 14 below, the
same bearing is tested again, at diﬀerent levels of tolerance, but with change in rotational
velocity instead of perturbation size along the x-axis.

Here it is clear that the tolerance does indeed have an eﬀect on the results from perturbation
theory. But evidently decreasing the tolerance to a value lower than tol = 10−5 doesn’t
yield any additional improvement to the model, and all analyses carried out from this point
forward are therefore using that as the tolerance for numerical solutions. Compared to the
very low tolerance needed for Allaire’s method, this marks another signiﬁcant advantage of
perturbation theory.
Before the perturbation theory method can be utilised the way it’s implemented, the nec-
essary mesh density of the discretized pads needs to be determined. To which it is worth
noting that this method has one signiﬁcant drawback, as compared to Allaire’s method: the
need for a uniform mesh. The user of the TPJB programme has the option of switching
38
==PAGE:50==
between a uniform or non-uniform mesh, the ﬁrst being a remnant of the programme’s older
versions where that was the only option. The possibility of a nonuniform mesh was intro-
duced by Mønsted, as described in [2], and made the mesh more dense around any injection
oriﬁces for hybrid lubrication. Increasing the accuracy of the calculations when using hybrid
lubrication, but keeping down computational demand, which would otherwise accompany a
uniform mesh with high enough density to give equally accurate results.
Because of the ﬁnite diﬀerence method used to determine the derivatives of the ”new” pres-
sure at each node, due to perturbation, as described in the previous Section 5.3, the nonuni-
form mesh doesn’t work with this method. It is believed that the uneven distance between a
node and its two adjacent nodes, at the proximity of an oriﬁce, was the cause of the resulting
inaccuracies, as the resulting distribution of both ﬁrst and second order derivatives of the
pressure distribution showed immense spikes at these locations, not unlike discontinuities.
Later, four diﬀerent bearing conﬁgurations will be tested, ﬁrst with conventional and then
with hybrid lubrication. With hybrid, they’ll all be tested with a single centered oriﬁce in
each pad, the convergence study on the eﬀect of mesh density on the dynamic coeﬃcients
were therefore conducted for both conventional, and hybrid lubrication with a single centered
oriﬁce and three diﬀerent injection pressures Pinj.
20

Figure 15: Analysis of dynamic coeﬃcients for 4-pad LBP, THD determined by perturbation
theory, with change in mesh density.
The convergence analyses in Figure 15 above, shows that while the dynamic coeﬃcients of
a conventional TPJB with zero injection pressure converge at a half-pad mesh with pc = 30
nodes in the ¯z-direction, a TPJB with hybrid lubrication is another story. With an injection
pressure the dynamic coeﬃcients don’t converge until around pc = 60, likely due to having
made the mesh uniform to enable perturbation theory. This means that a half-pad mesh of
(pl + 2) × (pc + 1) = 122 × 61 nodes is necessary for accurate results when analysing TPJBs
with hybrid lubrication, while a half-pad mesh of (pl + 2) × (pc + 1) = 62 × 31 nodes should
be suﬃcient for conventional TPJB analyses.
It was a concern, that the necessity of a uniform mesh would diminish the computational
savings expected by the perturbation theory, compared to Allaire’s perturbation method.
While this isn’t at all the case for conventional TPJBs, it might be for TPJBs with hybrid
39
==PAGE:51==
lubrication.
Nevertheless this method will be used moving forward, with the previously
mentioned mesh densities for TPJBs with and without hybrid lubrication respectively. As a
ﬁnal comparison between Allaire’s perturbation method and perturbation theory, the stiﬀ-
ness and damping matrices for a 4-pad LBP THD bearing are presented in Appendix D. The
elements in each matrix are ﬁgures with the corresponding coeﬃcient plotted as a function
of rotational speed. And in every ﬁgure, the coeﬃcient is plotted for ISO and THD by means
of Allaire, as well as THD by means of perturbation theory. Considering the ﬁgures in those
matrices, there is no doubt that perturbation theory is the most accurate method of the two
presented in this section.
6
Solving the Rotor-Bearing System
With the TPJB dynamics determined by means of one of the two methods presented in
Section 5, and the global rotor-discs-bearings system assembled, its equation of motion is
solved by formulating the normal eignevalue problem:
Aφ = λφ
(103)
Wherein φ and λ are the eigenvectors and eigenvalues of the system respectively, and A is
a matrix build from the equation of motion, formulated as:
A =


I

(104)
Where M, C, G and K are the mass, damping, gyroscopic and stiﬀness matrices of the
global rotor-discs-bearings system, and I and 0 are identity and zero matrices of the same
size. Using the build-in Matlab function eig(A) then yields the eigenvectors and eigenvalues.
With the eigenvalues λ determined, some key parameters for the vibrational analyses can
be determined as well. The damping ratio ζ, as well as the undamped and damped natural
frequencies, ωn and ωd respectively, for mode i are deﬁned as:

The damped natural frequencies are used for plotting Campbell diagrams, with which the
critical speeds can be determined. The damping ratios are used for plotting stability maps,
which will reveal whether the system becomes unstable.

Oil Flow and Power Loss
When evaluating the performance or value of a rotor-bearing system, the oil and power con-
sumption are essential, often deciding factors to consider. The theory in this section was
implemented by Kurt in relation to [1], and is merely summarized here.
The oil ﬂow in and out of the oil ﬁlm between the rotor and a pad, can be summarized
in 3 terms: The ﬂow in Qin in the ¯y-direction, and the ﬂow out Qout and Qside in the ¯y- and
¯z-directions respectively. Assuming symmetry of course, so that the oil leaving the oil ﬁlm
at ¯z = 0 and ¯z = Lz, in the ¯z-direction can be assumed equal, Qside.
Figure 16: Flows in and out of a TPJB pad, ﬁgure from [1].
Based on the velocity proﬁles derived in relation to the modiﬁed Reynold’s equation in
Section 3.1, equations (17) and (18), the ﬂow in the ¯y- and ¯z-directions can be determined
by integrating those terms over the cross-sectional areas of the oil ﬁlm in the respective
directions.

between nodes in the ¯y- and ¯z-directions, at the edges where the ﬂow is calculated. Likewise
the viscosity µ is that of the nodes at the same edge.
Conservation of mass dictates that the ﬂow entering the oil ﬁlm must be equal to that
leaving. Such continuity in ﬂow, means that the ﬂow of oil, injected from the pad oriﬁce(ses)
in an actively lubricated TPJB, can be expressed as the sum of the ﬂows leaving the ﬁlm,
minus the ﬂow entering at the inlet:
Qinj = (Qout + 2Qside) −Qin
(122)
42
==PAGE:54==
For the case of conventional lubrication Qinject = 0, this is reduced to the ﬂow entering at
the inlet being equal to that leaving. As stated by Kurt in [1], for an ideal pump that means
the power needed to supply the injection ﬂow can be expressed as:
Powerinj = Pinj · Qinj
(123)
Where Pinj is the injection pressure.
The energy dissipated into heat due to the shear stress in the lubricant results in a loss
of power, appropriately named the ”power loss” and denoted as just Power. With the loss
of power being a deciding factor when chosing bearings in the industry, and the shear stress
being directly linked to the pressure of the oil ﬁlm the power loss in the TPJB with and
without active lubrication is of great interest.
As explained in [1], the shear stress in the oil ﬁlm is determined as:
τ = µ∂u¯y
∂¯x = 1

Couette
(124)
With the ¯Bj referance frame deﬁned as it is in Figure 2, ¯x = 0 on the pad surface and ¯x = −h
on the rotor surface, the Poiseuille term in equation (124) changes sign depending on where
in the ﬂuid ﬁlm it’s calculated. With the pressure distribution assumed constant across the
ﬂuid ﬁlm thickness, the shear stress is calculated at ¯x = 0 moving forward. Considering
Figure 17, the direction of rotation of the journal makes the Couette term positive all across
the pad. But as the pressure gradient ∂p(¯y,¯z)
∂¯y
is negative on the left side of the maximum in
Figure 17, and positive on the right, the shear stress is deﬁned as in equation (125) below:
τ(¯y, ¯z) = −∂p(¯y, ¯z)

8
Passive Bearing Selection
Following the approach taken in [1], this ﬁrst section with results has the goal of determin-
ing which bearing performs best without any active injection of oil. The argument for these
analyses being, that in case the active injection fails, the bearing should still be capable of
running without catastrophic failure.
The analyses will be conducted on four diﬀerent bearing conﬁgurations. Two 4-pad bearings,
one being ”load between pad” (LBP) and the other being ”load on pad” (LOP). And two
5-pad bearings, again with a LBP and a LOP. The speciﬁcs of each bearing are outlined in
Appendix E.

8.1
Campbell Diagrams and Stability Maps
The ﬁrst analyses conducted are to determine the stability of the diﬀerent bearings with-
out hybrid lubrication. Each bearing conﬁguration is run at diﬀerent ranges of velocities.
For the 4-pad bearings, the minimum velocities (2000 and 2900 RPM for LBP and LOP
respectively) are based on the convergence issues at lower values, also reported in [1]. While
similar convergence issues appeared for the 5-pad bearings, these were at signiﬁcantly lower
velocities. The chosen minimum velocities for the 5-pad bearings here, are based on the devi-
ations the results from perturbation theory showed with respect to the results from Allaire’s
method in the validation of the former, discussed in Section 5.4. These minium velocities
(1300 and 1900 RPM for LBP and LOP respectively), ensures that no dynamic coeﬃcent
from perturbation theory deviates with more than 2% from the corresponding from Allaire’s
method, under the conditions used in the validation (isothermal, 62 × 31 nodes half pad
mesh, tol = 10−3). All bearings are tested to a maximum rotational speed of 15000 RPM.
Bearing type
Rotational speed range
4-pad LBP
2000 RPM to 15000 RPM
4-pad LOP
2900 RPM to 15000 RPM
5-pad LBP
1300 RPM to 15000 RPM
5-pad LOP
1900 RPM to 15000 RPM
Table 4: Table of rotational speed ranges, at which each bearing is analysed.
The Campbell diagram for the 4-pad LBP, as visible in Figure 19, shows that none of the
modes lie along the 1X line (synchronous speed). Though at high rotational speeds, two of
them are fairly close. The critical speeds at which a mode crosses the 1X line, occurs at
around 2500 RPM, 13950 RPM and 14800 RPM. It also looks like more critical speeds might
occur below 2000 RPM, but due to the converging issues of the program at such velocities,
this couldn’t be conﬁrmed. Overall the 4-pad LBP appears to hold up well though, and the
dynamics at the critical speeds will be investigated further in the next section.
Similarly to that of the 4-pad LBP, the Campbell diagram for the 4-pad LOP looks de-
scent, as presented in Figure 20. Again, none of the modes lie along the 1X line, though
some are close to it at the highest velocities. There are three critical speeds in this range,
at 8250 RPM, 8425 RPM and 13860 RPM respectively. As with the 4-pad LBP, it appears
there might be more at velocities below 2900 RPM, which again couldn’t be investigated due
to the convergence issues of the programme. There also appears to be another just above
15000 RPM, as a mode is so close to the 1X line it almost looks like it crosses at this scale.
But with 15000 RPM being the goal velocity of this thesis, the behaviour above this velocity
is not of interest. The 4-pad LOP also appears to hold up well, and its dynamics under
critical speeds will also be investigated further in the next section.
In [1] it was illustrated how both of the 5-pad bearings under isothermal conditions had
45
==PAGE:57==
modes running along the 1X line, and were discarded for that very reason. Now, for ther-
mohydrodynamic conditions, the campbell diagrams in Figures 21 and 22 shows that while
the tendency is very similar at lower rotational speeds, it’s very diﬀerent at the higher. As
one would expect with the THD aspects of the programme having a larger eﬀect at higher
velocitites.
While it’s deﬁnitely not as easy to discard the 5-pad bearings based on this, as it might have
been under isothermal conditions, they do still have more modes lying along the 1X line in a
larger range of velocitites than any of the 4-pad bearings. The issue posed by a mode lying
along the 1X line, is that the bearing system in that case is close to a resonance frequency
in that entire range of velocities. Given that both 5-pad bearings have multiple modes lying
along the 1X line from the very lowest velocities, 1300 RPM and 1900 RPM respectively, all
the way to 6000 RPM is suboptimal.
As was the case for ISO, the stability maps for THD in Figures 19 to 22 shows no damping
ratio crossing the 0-line. So none of the bearings would lead to an unstable rotor-bearing
system. But again, the stability needs to be further investigated at the critical speeds.
As with the isothermal conditions investigated in [1], only the 4-pad bearings will be in-
vestigated further, as the large range of rotational speeds in which both 5-pad bearings have
multiple modes lying along the 1X line is enough to already deem those bearings inferior to
the 4-pad bearings.

8.2
Mode shapes
The mode shapes of the rotor-bearings system with the 4-pad LBP bearings, at the critical
speeds determined in the previous section is presented in Figure 23, running at 2500 RPM
two eigenfrequencies lie fairly close, as presented in Figures 23a and 23b. The similarities
between the two indicate that they are the forward and backward motion of the same mode
shape. In this mode shape, the displacement at the node with the bearings are very small,
which isn’t preferable as it doesn’t allow the bearing to do much damping of the system.
Instead the increasing amplitude of displacement towards the center of the shaft, gives the
modeshape an almost spherical appearance, looking similar to that of pure bending. The
damping ratios for this mode shape, are ζ = 0.067 and ζ = 0.069 respectively which is quite
low. But this could maybe be improved when introducing the hybrid lubrication.
The mode shapes presented in Figures 23c and 23d, at 13950 RPM and 14800 RPM respec-
tively, look very similar as well, again indicating that these are the forward and backward
motion of the same mode shape. In this mode shape there’s a nodal point at the center of
the shaft, as the amplitude of displacement here is close to zero. And the largest displace-
ments occur at the shaft ends, which unlike in the previously analysed modeshape allows
the bearings placed close to the shaft ends to supply the damping. The damping ratios for
this mode shape are ζ = 0.372 and ζ = 0.425 respectively, both above the 0.3 preferred value.
For the rotor-bearings system with the 4-pad LOP bearings, the mode shapes at the pre-
viously determined critical speeds are presented in Figure 24, for all three of them there
appear to be a nodal point at the center of the shaft with close to no displacement. The
mode shapes depicted in Figures 24a and 24c, at 8250 RPM and 13860 RPM respectively,
both have the largest displacements at the shaft ends, allowing the bearings to supply the
damping, and the damping ratio for the two mode shapes are ζ = 0.493 and ζ = 0.357
respectively.
The characteristically nonsymmetrical third mode shape, depicted in Figure 24b at 8425
RPM, doesn’t have the largest displacement at the shaft ends, but somewhere between the
center and the ends of the shaft, still allowing the bearings to supply a lot of damping.
Though the damping ratio for this mode shape is just ζ = 0.220, it’s relatively close to the
preferred value of 0.3.
While it’s regrettable that the programme’s convergence issues at lower rotational speeds
restrict the analytical possibilities, both 4-pad bearings look descent so far. Especially with
the possibility of hybrid lubrication.

8.3
Unbalance response
Following the analytical structure of [1], the unbalance responses are also plotted for the
4-pad TPJBs. Figures 26 and 27 both consist of the following four subplots:
• Campbell diagram
• Unbalance responses with the unbalance placed on the disc closes to the shaft center
(node 9).
• Unbalance responses with the unbalance placed in both shaft ends (nodes 1 and 19) in
0◦phase.
• Unbalance responses with the unbalance placed in both shaft ends (nodes 1 and 19) in
180◦phase.
51
==PAGE:63==
The unbalance is plotted for three nodes - color coded as in Figure 25 below: The shaft end
(node 1) colored black, the TPJB (node 2) colored red and the disc closes to the shaft center
(node 9) colored blue. Each node is then plotted with three diﬀerent line-types, the dashed
line illustrates the XJ-deﬂection, the dotted line illustrates the YJ-deﬂection and the solid
line illustrates the largest deﬂection in the XY -plane, approximated as
p
X2
J + Y 2
J .
Figure 25: Placements of unbalances, ﬁgure from [1]
The unbalance response for the 4-pad LBP system is presented in Figure 26. In every plot,
for all nodes (colors), XJ- and YJ-displacements (dashed and dotted lines respectively) are
coincident, which is consistent with the symmetry in the XY -plane observed in the mode-
shapes from Figure 23.
The largest displacement occurs in the node where the unbalance is placed: The disc closest
to the center (node 9 - blue) for the second subplot, and the disc ends (node 1 - black) for
the third and fourth.
The overall largest displacement occurs in the second subplot, with the unbalance placed
in the disc closest to the shaft center (node 9 - blue), with about 48.94 µm at around 42.5
Hz, which is close to the critical speed for the mode shape illustrated in Figures 23a and
23b. As discussed in the previous subsection, the mode shape at this critical speed has the
largest deﬂection in the center of the rotor, so by placing the unbalance as close to the shaft
center as node 9, the overall tendency of the modeshape is enhanced and the displacement
magniﬁed.
The third and fourth subplots clearly show that with the unbalance placed in the shaft ends
(node 1 - black), the displacement is larger for the nodes further out towards the ends, and
increases with the increase in angular velocity.
The unbalance response for the 4-pad LOP system is presented in Figure 27. Unlike that of
the LBP system, the XJ- and YJ-displacements aren’t coincident, which makes sense given
that the mode shapes depicted in Figure 24 weren’t all symmetric in the XY -plane. The
second subplot depicts the unbalance response with the unbalance placed in the disc closes
to the center (node 9 - blue), and like for the LBP system it would appear as if there might
be a spike in displacement of that node somewhere just below the 50 Hz line, in line with
the speculations about the possible presence of a critical speed just below 2900 RPM. But
which due to the convergence issues can’t be conﬁrmed.
The rest of the subplot clearly shows close to no displacement of any of the nodes. Con-
sidering that all mode shapes presented in 27 seemed to have a nodal point of close to zero
displacement amplitude at the shaft center, placing an unbalance here will have little to no
eﬀect on the unbalance response.
With the unbalance placed at the shaft ends instead however, the tendency of all the mode
52
==PAGE:64==
shapes in Figure 24 is clearly enhanced, as the displacement is larger for the nodes closer
to the shaft ends, and in that the displacment of said nodes increases with the increasing
angular velocity. Similarly to that of the LBP system in Figure 26.

8.4
Oil ﬂow and Power loss
In Figure 28 the inlet ﬂow Qin and power loss Power, as described in Section 7, are plotted for
each pad in the 4-pad LBP system as functions of the rotational speed Ω. In both subplots
it almost appears as if only two functions are plotted. This is due to the fact that the pads 1
and 2 (at 45◦and 135◦respectively) as well as pads 3 and 4 (at 225◦and 315◦respectively)
are more or less coincident.
Analysing the inlet ﬂow ﬁrst, it comes as no surprise that it increases close to linearly with
the increase in rotational speed, as the ﬂow is to a large extend shear driven. As a volumetric
ﬂow like this is basically the velocity in ¯y times the cross sectional area of the ﬂuid ﬁlm in
the ¯z¯x-plane, the larger cross sectional area at the upper pads (1 and 2) naturally results in
a larger ﬂow than at the lower ones (3 and 4), as is also evident in the ﬁrst subﬁgure. With
that being the only argument for the diﬀerence between the ﬂow of the upper and lower
pads, one would expect that diﬀerence to diminish with an increase in rotational speed,
as the rotor moves toward the center of the bearing. But instead the diﬀerence seems to
increase a little with the increase in rotational speed.
The power loss as presented in the second subﬁgure, also increases with the rotational speed,
as one would expect with the increase in pressure. The diﬀerence in powerloss between the
upper and lower pads, is like with that of the inlet ﬂow due to the external load on the
rotor pulling it towards the lower ones. This increases the pressure in the oil ﬁlm at the
lower pads, resulting in the power loss at those being consistently higher than in that at the
upper. As mentioned previously, the rotor will move towards the center of the bearing as the
rotational speed increases, because the pressure diﬀerence between the oil ﬁlms at the lower
and upper pads increases. This increase in pressure diﬀerence with the increasing rotational
speed is also visible on the second subplot, as the diﬀerence between powerloss at the lower
and upper pads clearly increases.
In Figure 29 the inlet ﬂow and power loss are plotted for each pad in the 4-pad LOP system.
As with the LBP Figure, here it almost appears as if only three functions are plotted, due
to the pads at 0◦and 180◦being close to coincident. With three distinct regressions in
each subplot, as opposed to just two in those of Figure 28, this conﬁguration is clearly less
symmetric. The overall behavior is the same however: the ﬂow increases with the increase
in rotational speed for all three pads, and the ﬂow is consistently higher at the upper pads
than at the lower ones. The power loss is consistently higher for the lower pads than for the
upper ones, and also increases with the rotational speed - moreso for the lower pads than
for the upper ones.
While the exact values for the oil ﬂow and power loss, for all the pads of the diﬀerent
bearing conﬁgurations diﬀers a little, the overall tendency, as well as totalt oil ﬂow and
power loss, are roughly the same.

8.5
Conclusion
Due to the large range of rotational speeds, where the 5-pad bearings, both LBP and LOP,
had modes lying along the 1X line in the Campbell diagrams of Figures 21 and 22, those
were discarded early on. Leaving only the 4-pad bearings as viable options.
By analysing those further, it was discovered that, the mode shapes at the critical speeds of
the 4-pad LBP bearing were both symmetrical in the XY -plane. One was practically pure
bending, with a very low damping ratio. While the other had a more optimal damping ratio.
The 4-pad LOP bearing turned out to have a mode shape at one of its three critical speeds,
which wasn’t symmetrical in the XY -plane. But all three mode shapes had suﬃciently high
damping ratios.
56
==PAGE:68==
9
Varying injection pressure
The structure of analyses in the previous section, has closely followed that of the corre-
sponding section in [1]. This was intentionally done so to enable easier comparison between
the results from THD with those from ISO, and made sense when the results from those
THD analyses turned out to be so similar to the ISO results from [1]. While the intent is to
continue in the same manner for the sake of comparability, the approach in this perticular
section diﬀerentiates itself somewhat, due to limitations of the numeric methods used in
solving the energy equation.
Having determined that the 4-pad LBP bearing was the best TPJB for conventional lu-
brication with isothermal conditions, as it was for thermohydrodynamic conditions in the
previous section, Kurt continued to optimize the bearing conﬁgurations by analysing with
hybrid lubrication, with injection pressures varying from 20 to 300 bar. A range of injection
pressure which simply can’t be evaluated in the new model.
It was discovered, that when running the program at a constant angular velocity of the
journal, there was a limit as to how high the injection pressure could get before the solution
failed to converge. And there is a destinct correlation between the rotational speed and
the maximum allowable injection pressure. In Appendix F some plots of the pressure and
temperature distribution on pad 4, is presented at diﬀerent injection pressures at 2000 RPM
and 15000 RPM respectively. It is believed that the problem resides in the large pressure
diﬀerence, between the oil coming from the oriﬁce and the oil in the surrounding ﬂuid ﬁlm,
present when the injection pressure is high and/or the rotational speed is low. When comput-
ing the derivatives of the pressure distribution numerically, necessary for solving the energy
equation, it’s possible that extreme values may have occured. Similarly to what happended
for the second order derivatives of the pressure in the perturbation method when using a
nonuniform mesh.
This limited the options for analyses to overall low injection pressures, the highest of which
couldn’t be tested at the lower rotational speeds. The injection pressures used in testing the
hybrid lubrication, and the rotational speed range in which they are evaluated are as follows:
Injection pressure
Rotational speed range
No injection
2000 RPM to 15000 RPM
1 bar
3000 RPM to 15000 RPM
2 bar
3000 RPM to 15000 RPM
4 bar
3000 RPM to 15000 RPM
8 bar
6000 RPM to 15000 RPM
16 bar
10000 RPM to 15000 RPM
Table 5: Table of rotational speed ranges, at each injection pressure analysed for the 4-pad
TPJB with LBP.
57
==PAGE:69==
Besides the lower injection pressure, the fewer levels of pressure tested, and the smaller range
of rotational speeds at which each level of injection pressure is tested, the analyses are carried
out similarly to how it was done in [1]. With just a single oriﬁce in the center of each pad.
9.1
Injection on all pads
The ﬁrst analyses conducted with varying injection pressure, was with injection on all pads
simultaneously. In Figure 30 the resulting Campbell diagram and stability map are presented.
The modes for the various injection pressures follow each other to a large degree, in both
subplots, suggesting that the diﬀerence in pressures aren’t big enough. And they seem to
follow the overall tendency of the modes for no injection as well, but to a signiﬁcantly smaller
degree. Interestingly the modes for injecting oil appear to close in on the 1X line at the lower
velocities, but move away from it at the higher velocities, when compared to the modes for
no injection. With the goal of running at high velocities this might be favorable, but may
cause problems during start-up. The stability map clearly shows that injecting oil doesn’t
result in critical instabilities, as none of the new modes cross the zero-line.

Figure 31 shows the approximated maximum unbalance response for node 1 (shaft ends),
at the diﬀerent levels of injection pressure. The second subplot is the unbalance response
with the unbalance placed at the disc closest to the center. This shows that while the in-
58
==PAGE:70==
jection pressure has close to no eﬀect at the lower angular velocitites, the displacement is
clearly increased at the higher angular velocities. The same tendency is seen in the third
and fourth subplots, where the unbalance response is plotted for the unbalance placed at
the shaft ends, in and out of phase respectively. In those two plots however, the unbalance
responses for when injecting oil deviates a great deal more from those for no injection, push-
ing the unbalance even further beyond the dashed line which illustrates the amplitude limit
of AL = 22.36 µm as determined in Section 6.1.
Figure 32 shows the same four subplots, but for node 9 (shaft center). Here the tendency
is the same. Though with the unbalance placed near the center of the shaft, the largest
displacement occurs at 42.5 Hz, as discussed previously. Which is below the minimum rota-
tional velocity of 3000 RPM at which any injection pressure was tested. So it’s not possible
to determine whether the injection pressure might lessen that maximum displacement, or
worsen it as it does at the higher angular velocities. The latter seems more likely, as it’s
an apparent tendency that the higher the amplitude of displacement with no injection, the
bigger the increase in amplitude is with injection. And while this couldn’t be conﬁrmed, the
results so far have exhibited similar tendencies as the isothermal results in [1], wherein the
injection pressure also increases the displacement amplitude at the lower angular velocities.
While the increased vibrational amplitude came as no surprise, given that the same was
seen for the isothermal equivalents in [1], it’s still disappointing as the intend of the hybrid
lubrication is the exact opposite. To see if the fault lies with the fact that the oil is injected
through all pads simultaneously, the analyses are repeated in the next two subsections, where
that is not the case.

9.2
Injection on top pads (pads 1 and 2)
Figure 33 below shows the Campbell diagram and stability map for the same analysis as
in the previous subsection, but with injection on only the upper two pads (pads 1 and 2),
with the lower two remaining conventionally lubricated. The Campbell diagram in the ﬁrst
subﬁgure appears very diﬀerent when compared to the Campbell diagram for injection on all
pads. The modes for when injecting oil seem to follow the corresponding modes for no injec-
tion more closely. And while the modes also seem to close in on the 1X line with increasing
injection pressure for the lower velocities, with one of them crossing it at 8 bar injection
pressure. Similar to what was seen for injection on all pads. They don’t all appear to dis-
tance themselves from the 1X line at the higher velocities. Instead the majority of modes
seem to continue the trend of closing in on the 1X line with increasing injection pressure, in
the entire range of velocities with which they were tested.
The stability map shows the same tendency of the modes with injection more closely follow-
ing the modes with no injection, than what was seen for injection on all pads. And again,
no mode crosses the 0-line.

Figure 33: Campbell diagram and stability map for 4-pad LBP, with varying injection pres-

In Figure 34 the unbalance responses for node 1 is presented, for injection on only the upper
two pads (pads 1 and 2). The second subplot is again the unbalance response for node 1
with the unbalance placed on the node closest to the shaft center, which looks very similar
61
==PAGE:73==
to the corresponding subplot for injection on all pads in Figure 31. The same can be said
about the third and fourth subplots, depicting the unbalance response of node 1 for the un-
balance placed in the shaft ends in and out of phase respectively. Two major diﬀerences are
apparent in all three subplots, when compared to the corresponding analyses for injection
on all pads. At the lower velocities the injection of oil again has close to no eﬀect on the
displacement amplitude, but at the higher velocitites: the injection of oil doesn’t increase
the amplitude of displacement nearly as much for injection on all pads. Secondly, while
the injection of oil at low pressure does seem to increase the amplitude of displacement,
especially at the velocities where the amplitude is already high for no injection. The sub-
sequent increase in injection pressure appears to lower that amplitude. So much so, that
at 16 bar of injection pressure the amplitude of displacement is actually lower than that
of no injection, for a large range of velocities, in all subplots. This is the opposite aﬀect
of increasing the injection pressure, compared to what happened for injection on all pads,
where the increase in injection pressure consistently increased the amplitude of displacement.
In Figure 35 the unbalance responses for for node 9 are plotted, with the unbalance placed
in the same three diﬀerent conﬁgurations. And the same similarities and diﬀerences between
the unabalance responses of node 1, for injection on all pads and on just the upper pads, are
apparent here for node 9: The injection pressure has close to no eﬀect on the displacement
at lower velocities, and increases the amplitude of displacement at the higher velocitites.
Though destinctly less than for the case of injection on all pads. And again, while injecting
oil with low pressure does increase the amplitude of displacement at the higher velocitites,
increasing that pressure appear to reverse the eﬀect.
It’s a positive result that the amplitude of displacement of the tested nodes can now be
diminished by injecting oil. But regrettable that the same tendency couldn’t be tested at
the lower velocitites, where the maximum displacement appears for the unbalance response
of node 9. And that the analyses couldn’t be conducted at even higher injection pressures, to
see if the tendency continued as far as getting the amplitude of the maximum displacements
under the AL = 22.36 µm amplitude limit.

9.3
Injection on bottom pads (pads 3 and 4)
Figure 36 below shows the Campbell diagram and stability map for the same analysis as
in the previous subsections, but with injection on only the lower two pads (pads 3 and 4),
with the upper two remaining conventionally lubricated. The Campbell diagram in the ﬁrst
subplot looks more like the corresponding for injection on all pads, than the corresponding
for injection on the upper pads only did. The same trends occur: With the modes for oil
injection lying closer to the 1X line at lower velocities compared to those of no injection,
to then move further away from the 1X line than the modes with no injection at higher
velocitites. One signiﬁcant diﬀerence, between the Campbell diagram for injection on only
the lower pads, and that for injection on all pads, stands out however: For injection on all
pads the modes for all injection pressures were more or less coincident. Though the act of
injecting oil through the pad oriﬁces seemed to make a diﬀerence, the pressure with which
this was done didn’t appear to have much eﬀect what so ever. Here, for injection on only
the lower two pads, there are a clear distinction between the modes at 1 bar pressure and
those at 16, as well as the pressures in between. Other than that, there’s not much change
going from injection on all pads simultaneously, to injection on only the lower two pads. The
stability map in the second subplot again shows that no mode crosses the zero-line, meaning
none of them become unstable.

In Figure 37 the unbalance responses for node 1 is presented, for injection on only the lower
two pads (pads 3 and 4). As with the Campbell diagram in the ﬁrst subplot, discussed
previously, the unbalance responses for node 1 in the subsequent three subplots are very
similar to the corresponding subplots for injection on all pads in Figure 31. The injection
pressure has little to no eﬀect on the displacement at lower velocities. And while the eﬀect
at the higher velocities aren’t quite as large as with injection on all pads, the tendency of
the injection pressure having the largest eﬀect at the velocitites where the displacement is
already large without injection remains. And so does the tendency of the increasing injection
pressure resulting in increasing amplitude of displacement.
In Figure 38 the unbalance responses for node 9 is presented, for injection on only the lower
two pads (pads 3 and 4). When comparing with the corresponding unbalance responses for
injection on all pads, in Figure 32, the story is the same as when comparing the unbalance
responses of node 1.
Where the unbalance responses of node 1 and 9 when injecting oil on only the upper two pads,
as they were presented in the previous subsection, did appear to lower the vibrational am-
plitude of displacement when increasing the injection pressure high enough. The same can’t
be said when injecting oil on only the lower two pads. Instead the amplitude of displacement
increases, with the increase in injection pressure, for all unbalance responses presented here.
But the Campbell diagram from the previous subsection, for injection on only the upper two
pads, clearly showed the increase in injection pressure moving several modes critically close
to the 1X line, with one of them clearly crossing it. Which isn’t the case for injection on
only the lower pads. Based on the unbalance responses alone, the conﬁguration with varying
injection, out of the ones tested, with the most promising results is deﬁnitely the one with
injection on only the upper two pads. As that is the only conﬁguration where the hybrid
lubrication shows any sign of improving the vibrations. But considering the Campbell dia-
grams as well, the optimal conﬁguration isn’t so clear. One fact has become undoubtedly
clear for all conﬁgurations however: To truly test the eﬀectiveness of hybrid lubrication, and
to determine the optimal system of active control hereof, the tests need to be conducted with
a larger range of pressures, all at the same range of rotational speeds. All the unbalance
response plots, presented in this Section, can be inspected in further detail in Appendix G,
where they are presented again in a larger format.
