While the optimal conﬁguration of hybrid lubrication can’t be determined for the thermohy-
drodynamic TPJBs considered in this thesis, due to the limitations of the programme when
injecting oil through the pad oriﬁces. Kurt concluded in [1], that the optimal conﬁguration,
under isothermal conditions, was with injection on the lower two pads only. Assuming the
same would be concluded for THD, if the tests could be conducted at a big enough range
of injections pressures. One can continue analysing on said conﬁguration, by considering
the power loss and oil ﬂow into the pads, at the diﬀerent levels of injection pressure. For
good measure, the powerloss per pad is presented in Figure 39, and the inlet ﬂow per pad
is presented in Figure 40. Regretably though, without a large enough range of injection
pressures, nothing useful can be concluded based on those ﬁgures. And they are included
solely to underline that fact.

==PAGE:79==
More interesting is it to consider the injection ﬂow Qinj at the upper and lower pads (1 & 2,
and 3 & 4 respectively), as presented in Figure 41 below. As they might give a clue as to why
an injection pressure of 16 bar isn’t big enough to have a signiﬁcant eﬀect on the damping.
Considering the ﬁrst subﬁgure, it clearly shows the injection ﬂow at the upper pads for all
levels of injection pressure dropping, with the increasing rotational speed. The fact that the
injetion ﬂow here never gets above zero, retaining a back ﬂow of increasing magnitude with
the increasing rotational speed, for all values of injection pressure, is due to there being no
injection of oil on the upper two pads. As such, the increasing pressure in the oil ﬁlm, with
the increase in rotational speed, will naturally decrease the injection ﬂow further below zero.
When considering the second subplot for the lower two pads, where oil is indeed injected, the
tendency of a backﬂow is the same for the lower injection pressures. But by increasing the
injection pressure enough, a positive injection ﬂow can be established. This positive injection
ﬂow isn’t established however, with any of the tested injection pressures below 16 bar. And
even at 16 bar, it barely makes it above the zero line, and crosses back beneath it, recreating
a backﬂow into the oriﬁce, at around 13000 RPM. This goes to show, that to retain a positive
injection ﬂow, high enough to have a signiﬁcant eﬀect on the system dynamics, one might
need a very high injection pressure. Something which couldn’t be achieved in the analyses
of this thesis.

10
Conclusion
This project set out to modify the state of the art TPJB programme, to consider dissipation
of energy into heat. With that, all the analyses conducted for the isothermal equivalent
in [1] were to be repeated, to evaluate whether the assumption of isothermal conditions is
justiﬁable when considering diﬀerent tilted pad journal bearings. To that end the project
was somewhat successful.
The programme was successfully modiﬁed, so that the viscosity of the lubricant, where ever
it appeared in the programme, was dependant on the temperature of the lubricant. And
68
==PAGE:80==
the temperature distribution in the oil ﬁlm was determined by means of solving the energy
equation. But making the TPJBs thermohydrodynamic, as opposed to isothermal, resulted
in convergence issues with Allaire’s perturbation method. Necessitating unreasonably ﬁne
pad mesh/discretisation and low tolerances for the numerical solutions. In response to this,
perturbation theory was implemented as an alternative method for determining the dynamic
coeﬃcients of the bearings. Though moving the project beyond its original scope, this was
deemed necessary, and enabled the bearing dynamics to be evaluated with reasonable com-
putational demand.
The implementation of perturbation theory however weren’t without problems. Determin-
ing the ﬁrst and second derivatives of the pressure numerically, with respect to the two
dimensions ¯y and ¯z, resulted in devastating peaks around the pad oriﬁces, when using a
non-uniform mesh. Leading to the necessary disposal of the non-uniform mesh, which had
otherwise saved valuable computational time.
With the entire TPJB programme functioning as intended, the project could continue with
the vibrational stability analyses conducted for the isothermal counterparts in [1]. Here it
could be concluded that the 4-pad TPJB with LBP was the best candidate for a passive
bearing with conventional lubrication, as it had been for the isothermal case. And though
the conclusion for the passive bearing selection had been the same for THD as for ISO,
there were clear diﬀerences in both Campbell diagrams and unbalance responses for tested
bearings. And even some of the modeshapes for the critical speeds looked slightly diﬀerent.
The biggest diﬀerence between THD and ISO appeared when testing for hybrid lubrica-
tion with varying injection pressure. The diﬀerence didn’t lie in the results however, as
much as in which results could be achieved. As it turned out: the implementation of the
energy equation had further limited the programmes capabilities, such that when running
at a certain rotational speed, there was a limit to the level of injection pressure at which
the calculations could be evaluated. While the hybrid lubrication had been tested for ISO
in [1] in a range of 20-300 bar injection pressure, the highest pressure at which the same
analyses could be conducted for THD was 16 bar, and that was at a minimum rotational
speed of 10000 RPM. To test at lower rotational speeds, the injection pressure had to be
lowered accordingly. Though some clear eﬀects of the hybrid lubrication were apparent, the
limited range of injection pressure proved devastating. As it was impossible to fully evalu-
ate the optimal conﬁguration of pressures to improve the vibrational stability of the TPJB,
or regulate at which velocitites said pressures should be used under active hybrid lubrication.
The ﬁnal conclusion is that the diﬀerences between THD and ISO are too big, especially at
the higher rotational speeds, to ignore the thermal aspects of the ﬂuid mechanics. At the
lower velocities, the hybrid lubrication couldn’t be tested, but here the similarities between
THD and ISO under passive conventional lubrication are indisputable, and it can be assumed
the same goes for both passive and active hybrid lubrication. Though the thermohydrody-
namics is concluded to be signiﬁcant, the limits set by the way it’s implemented means the
necessary analyses to determine the optimal bearing conﬁgurations with varying injection
pressure can’t be conducted.
69
==PAGE:81==
11
Future work
Following the conclusion of this master’s thesis, there are a couple of things that could be
added to the TPJB programme, or simply improved upon. Things that if addressed in future
projects may improve the overall workings of the programme.
The ﬁrst thing worth mentioning is the problems with convergence of the solution to the
energy equation, when working with hybrid lubrication. Though the programme worked
as intended for passive conventional lubrication, the problems that occurred in the end of
the project, with the limits to the pressure of the injected oil in hybrid lubrication, were
devastating. By working to alleviate this problem, so the bearing could be analysed with
much higher injection pressure, in the full range of operational velocities, a proposal on how
to conﬁgure the injection pressure actively may be formulated, as to improve the damping
of the system in the best possible way.
Speaking of hybrid lubrication.
In this project only the diﬀerence in injection pressure
was tested, with a single centered oriﬁce in each pad. For future tests, it would be worth
looking in to the eﬀect of varying the diameter of the injection oriﬁce, the number of oriﬁces
in each pad and their positions.
With the temperature eﬀect on the ﬂuid implemented, a natural next step would be to
implement temperature eﬀects on the solids of the systems. Whether or not this would
have any eﬀect on which bearing or conﬁguration hereof one would choose as the optimal,
is impossible to say. But the change in temperature would deﬁnitely have an impact on
the geometry of the solid objects in the model, and by extend may aﬀect the dynamics of
the model. Implementing such aspects into the TPJB programme would enable it to more
closely represent reality.
70

Appendix C

uated for the 4-pad TPJB with LBP, by means of Allaire’s perturbation method and
perturbation theory. As described in Section 5.4. The resulting coeﬃcients are com-
pared, and the deviation of the latter from the former are presented in percentages.

Table 13: Dynamic coeﬃcients comparison for a 5-pad LOP isothermic passive bearing at diﬀerent velocities.
82
==PAGE:94==
Appendix D
The dynamic matrices presented in this Appendix have ﬁgures as elements. The ele-
ments in each matrix are ﬁgures with the corresponding coeﬃcient plotted as a function
of rotational speed. And in every ﬁgure, the coeﬃcient is plotted for ISO by means of
Allaire with a half-pad mesh of (pl+2)×(pc+1) = 62×31 and tolerance of tol = 10−3,
THD by means of Allaire with a half-pad mesh of (pl + 2) × (pc + 1) = 62 × 31 and
tolerance of tol = 10−10, and THD by means of perturbation theory with a half pad
mesh of (pl + 2) × (pc + 1) = 122 × 61 and tolerance of tol = 10−5.
83

Appendix E
The bearing speciﬁcs used for the analyses in Sections 8 and 9, are presented in this
appendix. First for the 4-pad bearings with LBP and LOP, then for the 5-pad bearings
with LBP and LOP. These settings are purposefully identical to those of the bearings
tested in [1], with the exception of parameters related to the thermal eﬀect in the
lubricant. This was done to make the results from testing on these bearings for THD,
comparable to the results for the bearings tested for ISO in [1].
86
